**XREF terms (59 total):** vocabulary borrowed from neighbouring registries; see data package §2.2 for full inventory.


## Lexical and semantic analysis (T7.1)

**T7.1.1 — What are the primary Hebrew and Greek terms for this characteristic — and what do their root meanings reveal?**

Primary Hebrew terms and root meanings: **chesed** (H2617A) — root implies binding loyalty, covenant-fidelity; possibly related to the idea of steadfastness or bending toward; the most theologically laden term in the OT love vocabulary. **ahavah** (H0160) — root ahav (to breathe after, to desire) — implying love as a form of directed longing or desire. **racham** (H7355) / **rachamim** (H7356B) — root rechem (womb) — love as womb-like envelopment and tender compassion; the most somatically grounded root. **dod** (H1730G/H) — root unclear; used for lover/beloved in the Song — the erotic-relational vocabulary. Primary Greek terms: **agapē** (G0026) / **agapaō** (G0025) — of uncertain pre-classical etymology; LXX/NT coinage whose meaning is constituted by Scripture rather than importing a prior secular meaning (OBS-103-016). **phileō** (G5368) / **philia** (G5373) / **filos** (G5384) — root from philos (friend); classical Greek for natural affectionate love. Root meanings together reveal: love spans willed-covenant (chesed), longing-desire (ahavah), somatic-compassion (racham), erotic-beloved (dod), self-giving-willed (agapē), and natural-affection (phileō).

**T7.1.2 — What is the grammatical range of the primary term (noun, verb, adjective, participle) — and what does that range reveal about how the characteristic operates?**

Taking agapaō/agapē as the primary NT term: **agapaō** (verb, 344 occurrences) — love as an action, exercisable and commandable; the verbal form affirms love's volitional dimension. **agapē** (noun, 133 occurrences) — love as an entity, a reality that can be 'poured into hearts' (Rom 5:5), that 'never fails' (1Cor 13:8), that is the greatest (1Cor 13:13). **agapētos** (adjective/participle, 79 occurrences) — beloved, the passive recipient of love; love as constituting an identity-state in the object. Together: verb + noun + adjective-participle spans the full grammatical range: love as act (verb), love as reality/quality (noun), and love as identity-constitutive state (adjective). This three-part grammatical range confirms Q&A-016's finding that love is simultaneously act, disposition, and condition. For chesed: primarily noun (covenant-quality) with verbal cognate (chasad — to act with chesed) — the noun-primary form confirms love as a stable quality before it is an act.

**T7.1.3 — What is the semantic range of the primary term — across what breadth of meaning does it operate?**

Agapē's semantic range in the NT evidence: (a) divine love for humanity (Joh 3:16); (b) human love for God (Mat 22:37); (c) mutual love in community (Joh 13:34); (d) love of enemy (Mat 5:44); (e) disordered love (love of darkness — Joh 3:19; love of the world — 2Ti 4:10; love of the pre-eminent position — 3Jo 9). The semantic range spans divine, human-covenantal, communal, and misdirected forms. Chesed's range: divine faithfulness, human loyalty, the person-type (chasid), the failure of chesed (withdrawal, demand). The combined semantic range of the primary terms covers the full moral landscape — from the highest (God's love as his being) to the lowest (love misdirected toward darkness). This breadth is unique in the programme — no other characteristic has primary terms whose semantic range spans from the ontologically highest (divine nature) to the morally inverted (love of darkness).

**T7.1.4 — Does the vocabulary include terms that distinguish distinct aspects of this characteristic — disposition versus act, received versus given, condition versus quality?**

Yes — the vocabulary structure itself distinguishes these: **Disposition vs act**: agapē (noun — disposition) vs agapaō (verb — act); chesed (noun — the quality) vs chasad (verb — to act with chesed). **Received vs given**: agapētos (passive — received) vs agapaō (active — given); 'beloved of my soul' (Jer 12:7, received by Israel) vs 'I have loved you' (Jer 31:3, given by God). **Condition vs quality**: the lover-as-identity (chasid as person-type, OBS-103-026; filos as person-type, OBS-103-018) names the quality that has become a stable condition; the act-forms name love as an exercised capacity. The vocabulary is architecturally precise — it distinguishes what love is (quality/disposition), what love does (act), who love comes from (given), who love is directed toward (received), and who love constitutes (condition/identity).

**T7.1.5 — Does the vocabulary include a term for the structural opposite or absence of this characteristic?**

Yes — embedded as OWNER. H8130 sane (to hate, 139 active / 156 total) is the structural opposite within the registry (OBS-103-030). H8131 sena (hating, 1 active) is a second form. The hate vocabulary is not XREF but OWNER — the programme treats the structural opposite as constituent of the love vocabulary's boundary (OBS-103-019). Additionally: the philo-compound negative forms (afilarguros — not-money-loving; pseudadelfos — false-brother) name the absence of specific love-forms. The disordered love vocabulary names love's distorted presence rather than its absence. True lovelessness (absence without misdirection) is not prominently named — the data suggests the inner mechanism is always present and always directed at something; the question is always the object, not the presence or absence of love-capacity.

**T7.1.6 — Does the vocabulary include a person-type term — a term for the one who habitually possesses or exercises this characteristic?**

Yes — two primary person-type terms: (a) **H2623 chasid** (pious/godly one, 33 active) — the person-type of chesed. The chasid is defined by their inner character of covenantal love toward God (OBS-103-026). (b) **G5384 filos** (friend, 27 active) — the person-type of phileō. The filos is one whose inner orientation of loyal affection has become their defining relational identity (OBS-103-018). Additional person-type compounds: filagathos (lover of good — positive), filarguros (money-lover — negative), filotheos (God-lover), filēdonos (pleasure-lover), filautos (self-lover — via 2Ti 3:2). The philo-compound system generates person-types for each form of love (OBS-103-017). The programme has the richest person-type vocabulary in the programme for any single characteristic — the love-vocabulary directly generates who you are from what you love.

**T7.1.7 — Does the vocabulary include a supplication or seeking term — a term for the act of seeking this characteristic from another?**

The data contains supplication-for-love contexts but not a dedicated seeking term. Lam 3:22 (OBS-103-088): the community crying out for chesed in the midst of desolation is implicit supplication. The Psalms' consistent 'according to your chesed' (Psa 63:3, Psa 78:38) pattern is love-seeking as petition. Hos 3:5 (OBS-103-082): 'they shall come in fear to the Lord and to his goodness' — movement toward God's love/goodness as a seeking-act. However, a specific lexical term for 'seeking love' is not identified in the current data package. Gap: the seeking-term for love may exist in the broader corpus (the Psalms' petition-for-chesed formulae) but is not captured as a named group or anchor in R103's current structure.

**T7.1.8 — What does the LXX use of the vocabulary reveal about continuity or development of this characteristic across the Testaments?**

The key LXX observation from the data: the LXX translators used agapē/agapaō to translate both ahav and chesed, embedding the Hebrew covenantal sense of steadfast love into the Greek agapē vocabulary. This is historically significant — agapē in the NT carries the weight of the OT chesed through the LXX translation. When the NT says 'God so loved (ēgapēsen)' (Joh 3:16), it uses the term that the LXX used for God's chesed toward Israel. Continuity is established at the lexical level: the NT's agapē is not a new concept but the Hebrew chesed in Greek form. Gap: the NO_WORD_ANALYSIS flag (142 instances, OBS-103-156) limits the depth of LXX-analysis available from the data package. Full LXX lexical analysis is not possible from the current data.

**T7.1.9 — Is there a term newly coined in the NT period for this characteristic — and if so, what does that coinage reveal?**

Yes — agapē itself is effectively a NT-period coinage in its theological-central usage. The word exists rarely in classical Greek but carries no comparable weight; its meaning is constituted by its LXX/NT usage (OBS-103-016). The coinage-significance: the NT needed a word for love that was not pre-loaded with the Platonic eros tradition or the friendship-philia tradition — agapē fills this gap, its meaning entirely constituted by Scripture. This is analytically significant: agapē is the programme's strongest example of a characteristic whose vocabulary definition is wholly internal to the scriptural corpus. What agapē means cannot be determined by reference to classical Greek usage — it must be read from the LXX and NT contexts themselves.

**T7.1.10 — What does the full vocabulary arc reveal about this characteristic's complete semantic range?**

The full vocabulary arc reveals love as the programme's most semantically comprehensive characteristic. The arc spans: (a) **Ground of being** — 1Jo 4:8 (God is love — the ontological summit); (b) **Covenantal permanence** — chesed (never ceasing, Lam 3:22); (c) **Somatic compassion** — rachamim (womb-like movement, Isa 49:15); (d) **Erotic-intense** — dod, ahavah (Song vocabulary, OBS-103-041); (e) **Natural affection** — phileō (Joh 11:36); (f) **Volitional self-giving** — agapē (enemy-love, soul-laying-down); (g) **Object-directed attachment** — philo-compounds (love of money, God, pleasure, brothers); (h) **Structural opposite** — sane (hatred). The arc is a spectrum from the ontologically highest (divine being) through the morally normative (covenantal chesed, commanded agapē) to the naturally affective (phileō, dod) to the morally disordered (love of darkness, self, money). No characteristic in the programme covers a comparable arc. Love is the vocabulary's outermost circle — encompassing the greatest range of inner-being phenomena under a single governing concept.


## Verse and literary interpretation (T7.2)

**T7.2.1 — What is the function of this characteristic's primary term within its primary verse — what role does it play in the sentence and argument?**

Identifying 1Jo 4:8 ('God is love') as the primary theological claim and Joh 15:13 as the primary ethical-definitional verse: In 1Jo 4:8: the predicate nominative construction ('God is love') makes love not merely an attribute of God but an identification — love as God's being. The verb 'is' (estin) establishes identity, not activity. In Joh 15:13: 'greater love has no one than this: that someone lay down his life for his friends' — love is the subject of a comparative superlative; the function is to name the outer limit of love by identifying its maximum expression (soul-giving). The primary term in both cases functions as the frame of reference against which all other love-instances are measured: Joh 15:13 defines love's maximum; 1Jo 4:8 defines love's origin. Together they establish the top and bottom of love's definitional range: what love ultimately is (God's being) and what love ultimately does (gives itself to death for the beloved).

**T7.2.2 — What literary form carries the primary verse evidence (narrative, psalm, wisdom, prophecy, epistle, apocalyptic) — and what does that form require for responsible interpretation?**

Love's evidence base spans every major literary form: (a) **Wisdom/Proverb** (Pro 10:12, 27:6): requires attention to the comparative and aphoristic structure — proverbs describe patterns, not absolutes; (b) **Psalm** (Psa 63:3, 16:11, 97:10): requires reading as first-person prayer/meditation — the psalmist's inner orientation, not universal dogma; (c) **Prophetic oracle** (Jer 31:3, Eze 16:8, Isa 49:15): the love-language is covenantal and often metaphorical (marital imagery); responsible reading holds the metaphorical dimension; (d) **Epistle** (1Cor 13, 1Jo 4, Rom 5): the most theologically discursive form — allows propositional claims about love's nature; (e) **Gospel narrative** (Joh 3:16, Luk 7:38, Joh 15:13): love embedded in narrative events requiring attention to the narrative's argument; (f) **Song/Poem** (Song of Songs): erotic love in a canonical wisdom-poem; the allegorical interpretive tradition and the literal reading must both be held. No single form dominates — love's evidence is multi-formal.

**T7.2.3 — What is the logical structure of key arguments in the verse evidence — what are the premises and conclusions?**

Three key argument structures: (a) **Causal** — 1Jo 4:19: 'we love because (hoti) he first loved us' — divine love is the causal ground of human love. 1Cor 13:13: 'the greatest of these is love' — superlative argument establishing love's primacy. (b) **Conditional** — Joh 14:15: 'if (ean) you love me, you will keep my commandments' — love as condition for obedience. Mat 5:44: 'love your enemies (command) so that (hopōs) you may be sons of your Father' — love as condition for identity-as-child-of-God. (c) **Comparative** — Joh 15:13: 'greater love has no one than this' — superlative-comparative establishing love's maximum. Psa 63:3: 'your steadfast love is better (tov) than life' — comparative valuing love above the highest natural good. The argument structures cluster around: love as cause, love as condition, and love as the superlative good. The data argues that love is causally prior, conditionally necessary, and comparatively supreme.

**T7.2.4 — What contextual setting carries the primary verse evidence (judicial, liturgical, covenantal, communal, eschatological) — and what does that setting reveal?**

Three dominant contextual settings: (a) **Covenantal** — the OT love vocabulary (chesed, ahavah, racham) is carried primarily in covenantal contexts (Sinai formula, Deuteronomy love-command, prophetic covenant-marriage imagery). This reveals love as the inner disposition of covenant — it is what the covenant relationship requires and produces at the inner-being level. (b) **Communal/ecclesial** — the NT love vocabulary (agapē in Paul and John) is carried in the context of the believing community (1Cor 13 in worship-chaos context; Joh 13:34 at the Last Supper; 1Th 3:12 in community growth prayer). This reveals love as the community's constitutive practice. (c) **Lament/petition** — Lamentations, Psalms of lament: love's permanence is specifically evidenced under conditions of suffering and judgment. This reveals that love's most definitive testimony is given precisely when circumstances deny it — chesed is most powerfully stated when experience is most desolate (Lam 3:22).

**T7.2.5 — Does any verse function as the primary anchor for this characteristic — the verse that most fully and directly expresses its essential character?**

Two verses function together as the primary definitional anchor for love in the data: (a) **Lam 3:22**: 'The steadfast love (chesed) of the Lord never ceases; his mercies never come to an end' — the OT's most concentrated statement of love's permanence under maximum adverse conditions. The theological claim is absolute: love does not cease. (b) **Joh 15:13**: 'Greater love has no one than this: that someone lay down his life for his friends' — the NT's primary definition of love by its maximum expression. Together they bracket the love vocabulary: Lam 3:22 defines love by its permanence (it never stops); Joh 15:13 defines love by its cost (it gives everything). If only one is chosen: Lam 3:22 is the more foundational — it states what love ultimately is (permanent, reliable, the ground of hope in desolation); Joh 15:13 states what love ultimately does.

**T7.2.6 — What does the primary anchor verse reveal that no other verse reveals?**

**Lam 3:22** reveals that love's most definitive affirmation comes in the moment of maximum absence — the desolation of Jerusalem, the silence of God, the evidence of judgment everywhere. No other verse places love's permanence claim at the precise moment when it is most empirically falsifiable. The verse does not argue for love's permanence from evidence of love's presence — it asserts permanence amid evidence of absence. This reveals that chesed in the OT is not an empirical observation (I observe that God has been loving) but a theological confession (I confess that God's love does not cease, even when it does not appear). This is love at its most epistemically demanding: the confession of love against the evidence of its apparent absence. No other verse in the data makes this claim with this evidence-structure.


## Somatic dimension — body significance, direction, deposit

How (and whether) the characteristic is grounded in the body — whether Scripture links it to specific body parts, the direction of body↔soul interaction, and any constitutional deposit produced over time.


### T2.6 — Body — Significance

**Prompt 1: Does the verse evidence link this characteristic to a specific body part?**

Extensively. Joy (Psa 16:11, OBS-103-112; Zep 3:17, OBS-103-094); grief (Song 5:6, OBS-103-062; Joh 11:36, OBS-103-149; 2Sa 1:26, OBS-103-095); rest/stillness (Zep 3:17, OBS-103-094; Psa 127:2, OBS-103-084); delight (Song 2:16, OBS-103-061; Pro 5:19, OBS-103-099); compassion-movement (Luk 15:20, OBS-103-057; 1Ki 3:26, OBS-103-105); yearning (Jer 31:20, OBS-103-117); anguish at separation (Song 5:6, OBS-103-062). Love's emotional palette is among the richest in the programme. Love does not suppress emotion — it generates, focuses, and intensifies the inner affective life.

**Prompt 2: If so, what is Scripture doing by making that link — is it emphatic, functional, expressive, indicative, or mediating?**

Produced by love: joy and fullness of pleasure (Psa 16:11, OBS-103-112) — love in the presence of the beloved generates joy. Rest and quieting (Zep 3:17, OBS-103-094) — being loved by God produces inner stillness. Yearning (Jer 31:20, OBS-103-117) — God's love for Ephraim expressed as inner yearning for the wayward. Grief (2Sa 1:26, OBS-103-095; Song 5:6, OBS-103-062) — love under loss produces grief as its evidence. Intoxication/delight (Pro 5:19, OBS-103-099) — erotic love produces a pleasure-state described as intoxication. Required by love: compassion-movement (Luk 15:20, OBS-103-057) — responsive love requires the inner organs to be moved; without the emotional movement, the love-act does not occur. The full range: love produces the most positive (joy, delight, rest) and the most painful (grief, yearning) affective states in the inner-being vocabulary.

**Prompt 3: If no body-part link is evidenced, note this explicitly.**

Love is not primarily emotional in the biblical data, though it has a rich emotional dimension. The evidence positions love as primarily dispositional/constitutional — an inner orientation that produces emotional states rather than consisting in them. Chesed is the clearest evidence: it persists without depending on emotional states (Lam 3:22 — 'the steadfast love of the Lord never ceases' despite Israel's devastating judgment). Agapē of enemies (Mat 5:44) operates where positive feeling is absent or where it must overcome negative feeling. The emotional states (joy, grief, rest, yearning) are love's products and evidence, not its constitutive substance. If love were primarily emotional, its commanded form ('you shall love') would be incoherent — emotions cannot be commanded. The commanded form confirms that love's primary operation is dispositional-volitional; the emotional dimension is real but secondary.


### T2.7 — Body — Direction

**Prompt 1: Where a body-characteristic link exists, which direction does it run — does the soul express through the body, does the body feed back to the soul, or does it run in both directions?**

Indirect evidence only. The conscience (suneidēsis) does not appear by name in R103's anchor verses. However: Lev 19:17 (OBS-103-114) — the prohibition on hating the brother 'in your heart' and the command to rebuke frankly — implies conscience-engagement: the inner recognition that hate is wrong and that honest love requires action. 2Cor 11:26 (OBS-103-067) and Gal 2:4 (OBS-103-068) — the false brother's concealed inner orientation — implies conscience is involved in the recognition of inner deception. The programme's suneidēsis registry (R026) co-occurs with love at Rom 8:29 (shared anchor). Gap: conscience's specific engagement with love is not explicitly evidenced in R103's anchor corpus. The conscience-love connection is inferred from the moral imperative structure of the love commands.

**Prompt 2: What is the consequence of that directionality for understanding the characteristic?**

The indirect evidence suggests conscience and love operate at the same moral depth but through different mechanisms. The conscience recognises the moral wrongness of hate-in-the-heart (Lev 19:17, OBS-103-114); love is what the conscience directs the person toward as the alternative. If conscience is the inner moral monitor, love is the inner moral orientation — the conscience identifies what violates love; love provides the positive orientation the conscience is protecting. Gap: this relationship is inferred, not directly evidenced in R103's anchor verses.

**Prompt 3: If no body-characteristic link is evidenced, note this explicitly.**

The evidence is not fully silent (see Q&A-053 and Q&A-062) but the direct naming of conscience in connection with love is absent from R103's anchor verse corpus. The co-occurrence of suneidēsis (R026) at the programme level (Rom 8:29 shared anchor) suggests a connection exists; its content is not determinable from the current data. Named gap: conscience-love explicit relationship not directly evidenced; inferred only.


### T2.8 — Body — Deposit

**Prompt 1: Does sustained operation of this characteristic leave a constitutional deposit in the body or its design — including DNA or generational consequence?**

The data provides extensive somatic expression evidence (Unit 7 — kiss, embrace, tears, womb-movement, inner organs) but the question of a lasting somatic deposit is distinct from somatic expression. Evidence relevant to deposit: Pro 3:3 — chesed written on the 'tablet of the heart' — the heart is a somatic location but this is more metaphorical inscription than physiological deposit. The inner organs (me'eka/splanchna) appear as the location of love's compassion-movement — this is a named somatic location but not clearly a lasting deposit. The womb-etymology of rachamim implies a bodily-constitutional rootedness of love. The evidence implies a deep somatic-constitutional rootedness of love without explicitly naming a body deposit. Gap: the precise question of lasting somatic deposit is not directly addressed. Flagging T5.7 dependency per instruction.

**Prompt 2: What evidence supports or contradicts this?**

The heart-inscription of chesed/faithfulness (Pro 3:3, OBS-103-090) — 'write them on the tablet of your heart' — is the closest evidence to a constitutional body-deposit: the heart-tablet metaphor implies that love's dispositional form is inscribed into the person's constitutional core. If this is treated as a deposit analogy, it reveals: (a) love's dispositional form (chesed) is meant to be a permanent inner-being inscription, not a transient orientation; (b) the heart as the deposit location is the deepest accessible somatic address in the love vocabulary; (c) the 'tablet' language implies durability — written, not merely felt. However, this is metaphorical language whose literal constitutional meaning is uncertain. Partial: the heart-inscription is the best available deposit-candidate in the data.

**Prompt 3: If the evidence is silent, note this explicitly. This finding feeds directly into T5.7.**

Body deposit in the explicit sense is not directly evidenced. The heart-inscription (Pro 3:3) is the closest candidate. The inner organs (me'eka/splanchna) are love's somatic location of compassion-movement but represent somatic expression rather than lasting deposit. For T5.7: if the body deposit at T2.8 is treated as absent or uncertain, T5.7 (Deposit Consequence) will receive not-applicable responses for all three prompts per the instruction's rule. This is flagged here. Recommendation: treat T2.8 as 'partially evidenced' (heart-inscription as metaphorical deposit candidate) and process T5.7 accordingly — neither full confirmation nor full N.


## Synthesis — what the language reveals


### Intra-tier syntheses

**SYN-INTRA-103-007 | T7** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

Love's evidential foundation is the most comprehensive in the programme. The primary terms (chesed, agapē/agapaō, racham/rachamim, ahavah, dod, phileō) cover the full constitutional range: willed-covenantal (chesed), Spirit-produced self-giving (agapē), somatic-compassionate (racham), intense-erotic (ahavah/dod), and naturally affective (phileō). The vocabulary structure itself distinguishes disposition/act/condition, received/given, and quality/person-type — making it architecturally precise. The semantic range of the primary terms spans from divine ontology (God is love) to moral inversion (love of darkness) — the widest semantic arc in the programme. The primary anchor is Lam 3:22 — love's most definitive affirmation precisely at the moment of maximum empirical falsification: a theological confession rather than an empirical observation. The LXX connection is partially evidenced (agapē as chesed-translated); full LXX analysis is limited by NO_WORD_ANALYSIS flags. The human science frameworks identified (attachment theory, virtue ethics) are named as Session D pointers but not elaborated — the instruction prohibits importing framework content not grounded in the data.


### Inter-tier syntheses

**SYN-INTER-103-006 | T1, T7** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

The evidential foundation confirms and specifies the definition at every point. The vocabulary analysis confirms the four-mode structure of the definition (Q&A-122, Q&A-123): chesed (covenantal-dispositional), racham (somatic-compassionate), ahavah/dod (intense-erotic), phileō/agapē (affective-natural and willed-unconditional). The grammatical range analysis confirms the tripartite definition (Q&A-123): verb (act) + noun (disposition) + adjective-passive (condition). The semantic range analysis confirms the vocabulary arc (Q&A-124, Q&A-131). The primary anchor (Lam 3:22 — Q&A-136, Q&A-137) confirms the definition's most counterintuitive feature: love at its most definitional is not evidenced by love's presence but confessed against its apparent absence. The definition says love is permanent, unwavering, faithful — and the evidential ground for this claim is a verse written in the context of Jerusalem's destruction. The evidential foundation is thus not primarily experiential but confessional — the definition's permanence-claim is a theological assertion, grounded in the character of God rather than in the consistency of human experience.

**SYN-INTER-103-011 | T2, T7** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

The evidential foundation supports and grounds the constitutional location claims. Each constitutional level identified in T2 has specific vocabulary evidence in T7: Spirit-level origin — Gal 5:22 (fruit of the Spirit), Rom 5:5 (poured into hearts through the Spirit); soul-level — Song 5:6 (soul failing), Mat 22:37 (all your soul), Joh 15:13 (lay down the soul); heart-level — Pro 3:3 (heart inscription), Rom 5:5 (into our hearts); mind-level — Mat 22:37 (all your mind), Joh 15:15 (knowledge disclosure); body-level — kiss/embrace/tears/inner-organ-movement vocabulary across multiple groups. The lexical range of the primary terms (Q&A-122 — chesed, racham, agapē, dod, phileō) corresponds precisely to the five constitutional levels: each term carries a different constitutional register as its primary habitat. The evidential foundation thus not only confirms the constitutional claims but shows them to be lexically grounded in the vocabulary's own structure.


---

# Chapter 5 — Interrelationships

**Registry 103 — Love** · *Connections and Research Pointers*

Cross-registry connections, structural relationships, vocabulary sharing, and shared verse anchors.

---

## Structural relationships (T6)


### T6.1 — Co-occurrence

**Prompt 1: Which adjacent characteristics appear most frequently alongside this one in the verse evidence?**

Top co-occurrences from §5 data (OBS-103-042 through OBS-103-051):
R023 compassion (196), R187 strength (105), R197 authority (69), R117 peace (68), R059 faith (62), R044 despair (55), R112 mind (52), R173 will (52), R019 calling (51), R182 Soul (50), R043 desire (49), R051 distress (49), R183 heart (47).
The pattern reveals three structural clusters: (a) **Love's relational cluster** — compassion (23), peace (117), kindness (99), mercy (111) — love co-occurs with its closest relational companions within C17, confirming cluster integrity; (b) **Love's constitutional cluster** — soul (182), heart (183), mind (112) — love is present in verses that name the inner constitutional locations; (c) **Love's theological cluster** — faith (59), calling (19), covenant (34) — love co-occurs consistently with the programme's foundational theological vocabulary. The surprise: strength (105) and authority (197) are co-occurrence #2 and #3 — love and power are structurally co-present in the data at high frequency.

**Prompt 2: What does the pattern of co-occurrence reveal about this characteristic's place in the inner-being landscape?**

Three surprising co-occurrences: (a) **Strength (R187, 105) and authority (R197, 69)** — love and power as the second and third highest co-occurrence registers. This is analytically unexpected — strength and authority are C20 cluster characteristics, not C17. The data suggests that love and power are not merely adjacent but structurally co-present in biblical contexts. The 1Cor 13 framework places love above gifts of power ('tongues of angels, gifts of prophecy') — the comparison implies they occupy the same evidential terrain. SD-026 (already raised) addresses this. (b) **Despair (R044, 55)** — despair appearing in the top 10 co-occurrences with love. The lament/petition pattern (despair crying to love for rescue) generates this co-occurrence. Love and despair are structurally linked as the inner state that appeals to love and the love that responds to despair. (c) **Guilt (R073, 32)** — less surprising given SD-028 (forgiveness-love chain) but notable that guilt appears in the top 25 — love and guilt are structurally related at the verse level through the forgiveness-love causal chain.

**Prompt 3: If no significant co-occurrence patterns emerge, note this explicitly.**

The co-occurrence pattern confirms the Relational Disposition primary dimension. Love co-occurs most densely with: compassion (the compassionate form of relational orientation), peace (the relational-outcome), faith (the trust-orientation toward God), calling (identity constituted relationally), and the soul/heart/mind (the constitutional locations of relational experience). This is the pattern expected for a Relational Disposition characteristic. The strength/authority co-occurrence adds texture (love operates in contexts of power) but does not complicate the primary classification — love remains relational disposition even when co-present with power vocabulary.


### T6.2 — Sequential Relationships

**Prompt 1: Does the verse evidence show this characteristic consistently preceding, following, or accompanying another characteristic in a sequence?**

The data is unusually rich in love's sequential relationships. Love as effect (something precedes it): (a) Received divine love → human love (1Jo 4:19, OBS-103-146); (b) Forgiveness received → love expressed (Luk 7:47, via SD-020); (c) Obedience → soul purification → sincere love (1Pe 1:22, OBS-103-124); (d) Spirit infusion → love in heart (Rom 5:5, OBS-103-126). Love as cause (love produces): (e) Love of God → commandment-keeping (Joh 14:15, OBS-103-140); (f) Love → all other Spirit-fruits (Gal 5:22, OBS-103-128 — if love is generative); (g) Love of money → craving → departure from faith → anguish (1Ti 6:10, OBS-103-132 — the disordered sequence); (h) Perfect love → displacement of fear (1Jo 4:18, via SP-103-029). The sequential data reveals love as both deeply downstream of divine action and richly upstream of the ethical and moral life. Love is the programme's most central sequential node.

**Prompt 2: What does the sequence reveal — is the relationship causal, developmental, or correlational?**

Love appears to occupy a **mediating and generative** position in the programme's sequential architecture. Mediating: it stands between received divine love (prior) and the ethical-moral life (subsequent). It is not the origin (God is the origin) but it is the indispensable middle — without love, no commandment-keeping, no Spirit-fruit, no community-identity. Generative: love heads the fruit-of-Spirit list (Gal 5:22) and defines the measure of all subsequent virtues (1Cor 13:13 — 'the greatest is love'). Love functions as the programme's organising characteristic at the ethical level: it is what the other Spirit-fruits emerge from and what the commandments resolve into. This is consistent with the registry description ('most foundational word') and DIM-103-001's call to map love's governing position relative to other characteristics.


### T6.3 — Causal and Constitutive Relationships

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic produce another characteristic in the verse evidence — and if so, which one, and by what mechanism?**

Yes — love is causally productive in the data at both the inner-being and the social levels: (a) **Love → joy** (Psa 16:11, OBS-103-112): love-in-God's-presence produces fullness of joy; (b) **Love → rest** (Zep 3:17, OBS-103-094): God's love produces inner quieting; (c) **Love → grief** (Song 5:6, OBS-103-062; 2Sa 1:26, OBS-103-095): love under loss produces grief — love constitutes the grief by making the beloved's absence unbearable; (d) **Love → obedience** (Joh 14:15, OBS-103-140): love of Christ produces commandment-keeping; (e) **Love → praise** (Psa 63:3, OBS-103-087): love expressed as response to chesed-above-life produces praise. Love does not merely accompany these states but appears to constitute or cause them. It is the programme's most causally productive characteristic.

**Prompt 2: Is this characteristic produced by another — and if so, which one?**

From the data: (a) Love may constitute or generate all Spirit-fruits (Gal 5:22, Q&A-105) — if love is the single fruit of which joy, peace, patience, etc. are expressions, then love partially constitutes those characteristics. (b) Forgiveness may constitute the condition from which love emerges (Luk 7:47 via SD-020) — if 'she loved because she was forgiven much,' forgiveness is constitutive of the love-response. (c) Compassion and love appear to be constitutively related — compassion may be love's form under the condition of the other's suffering (OBS-103-042, OBS-103-057). Gap: the precise constitutive relationships between love and compassion, mercy, kindness cannot be fully determined from Stage 2a evidence alone — these require Session D treatment. Partial: love-as-constituent-of-Spirit-fruits and forgiveness-as-constituent-of-love-response are the best-evidenced claims.


### T6.4 — Vocabulary and Root Sharing

**Prompt 1: Does this characteristic share vocabulary terms with other characteristics in the programme?**

Extensively — as noted in the XREF analysis (Unit 2, OBS-103-007 through OBS-103-015): (a) **Compassion (R023)**: racham/rachamim family shared — the compassion vocabulary is OWNER in R103 and primary in R023; (b) **Goodness (R067)**: tov/agathos family — H2896A-C tov is XREF in R103, OWNER in R067; (c) **Desire (R043)**: ahav verbal root (H0157G) is XREF in R103, primary in R043; (d) **Peace (R117)**: eirēnē family is XREF in R103; (e) **Hatred**: sane is OWNER in R103, also relevant to other registries. The term sharing ratio (0.701 — 96 of 137 terms shared, OBS-103-006) is among the highest in the programme. Love shares vocabulary with almost every adjacent registry in C17.

**Prompt 2: Does vocabulary sharing extend to root-level architecture — a shared root that generates terms across two or more characteristics?**

Yes — three root-level connections are analytically significant: (a) **Racham root** — spans love (103), compassion (23), and mercy (111) as a unified etymological family. The womb-compassion-love nexus (OBS-103-007, OBS-103-029) is a root-level connection — the same bodily root generates three distinct registry characteristics; (b) **Tov root** — spans love (103) and goodness (67), crossing cluster boundaries (C17 and implied goodness cluster). The goodness-as-love-expression connection (SD-009) is root-level; (c) **Ahav root** — spans love (103) and desire (43). H0157H (friend-ahev, OWNER R103) and H0157G (lover-ahev, OWNER R043 via XREF placement) are the same root, different programme assignments. Root-sharing confirms that the programme's registries are not sealed semantic units but connected through shared etymological architecture.

**Prompt 3: What does vocabulary sharing reveal about the conceptual relationship between characteristics?**

Vocabulary sharing at this density reveals that love is not a bounded standalone characteristic but the semantic centre of a vocabulary cluster. The characteristics surrounding love (compassion, mercy, kindness, goodness, desire, peace) share vocabulary with it because they are all expressions or dimensions of a common inner-being reality — oriented-attachment-to-the-good-and-to-persons. The registries do not name wholly distinct phenomena; they name distinguishable aspects of a single broadly relational inner-being reality. The practical implication: Session D synthesis for love cannot treat the other C17 characteristics as merely adjacent — they are lexically and conceptually continuous with love. The distinctions drawn in the programme's registry structure are analytical tools, not natural kinds.


### T6.5 — Distinctions

**Prompt 1: Which adjacent characteristic most closely resembles this one — and what precisely distinguishes them?**

The closest adjacent characteristic from co-occurrence data is compassion (R023, 196 shared verses). What distinguishes them from the data: compassion is love's responsive form under the condition of the other's suffering — the rachamim/womb-movement is activated by the beloved's need or distress. Love without suffering context does not require the racham-movement; love in the presence of suffering always produces it. Stated differently: compassion is the subset of love that is suffering-triggered; love is the broader orientation of which compassion is one mode (OBS-103-029, OBS-103-042). However: the data does not fully distinguish chesed from compassion at the verse level — both can be present simultaneously (Lam 3:22). The distinction is partial. Gap: the precise boundary between love and compassion requires dedicated Session D analysis of both registries' verse corpora together.

**Prompt 2: Where the evidence shows apparent overlap, what is the precise boundary?**

The racham/chesed overlap at Lam 3:22 ('his chesed never ceases, his rachamim never come to an end') is the programme's clearest example of the boundary difficulty — the two terms are paired in parallel without distinction. At the definitional level the boundary is: chesed = covenantal-faithful love (disposition of the covenant partner); rachamim = compassion-love (response to suffering). But at the verse level they co-occur so consistently that their experiential separation is unclear. Gap: the boundary is analytically drawn but evidentially porous. Session D is the appropriate resolution context.

**Prompt 3: Is the distinction between this characteristic and its nearest neighbour one of degree, kind, direction, or constitutional level?**

Between love and compassion: the distinction appears to be one of **trigger** rather than degree, kind, direction, or constitutional level. Both are inner dispositions of orientation toward another (same kind, same direction, similar constitutional level). What differs is the activating condition: compassion is triggered by the other's suffering; love can be present without suffering. This makes them the same characteristic differently activated, rather than different characteristics. This reading is consistent with SD-007 (compassion as love-responding-to-suffering hierarchy). However, the data does not definitively confirm this — it is an analytical interpretation of the co-occurrence and definitional data. Deferred to Session D for resolution.


### T6.6 — Shared Verse Anchors

**Prompt 1: Does any verse in this characteristic's evidence base also function as a primary anchor in another characteristic's study?**

Yes — 48 shared anchor verses identified (§6, OBS-103-050). Most significantly: Exo 34:6 (5 registries: anger, patience, faith, compassion, calling), Gal 5:22 (5 registries: patience, joy, goodness, peace, faith), 1Cor 13:4 (3 registries: patience, pride, kindness), Mic 6:8 (6 registries: goodness, kindness, will, humility, compassion, condemnation), Jer 31:34 (3 registries: guilt, thought, forgiveness). The 48-anchor shared map is the richest in the programme — love's evidential base is the most cross-registered in the corpus.

**Prompt 2: What does the shared anchor reveal about the relationship between the two characteristics?**

The shared anchor map reveals that love is the semantic core around which the programme's most structurally significant verses cluster. Exo 34:6 (shared with 5 registries) is the OT's most cross-registry verse — it names love/chesed as the centre of a five-attribute divine portrait. Gal 5:22 (shared with 5 registries) places love as the head of the Spirit-fruit list and all subsequent fruits as co-anchors. These are not random co-occurrences — they are structurally chosen: the programme anchored the most important characteristic-verses at love because love is the conceptual centre from which the adjacent characteristics derive their meaning. The shared anchor pattern confirms love's generative position (Q&A-105): love is what the surrounding characteristics are expressions of, not merely what appears alongside them.


### T6.7 — Dimensional Sharing

**Prompt 1: How many of this characteristic's confirmed analytical dimensions are shared with another characteristic in the programme?**

SD-026 (already raised) notes that C20 (strength/authority cluster) shares all 8 confirmed dimensions with love — co-occurrence in 69-105 verses. This is a programme-level dimensional sharing finding that requires Session D treatment. Within C17: all compassion, mercy, kindness, and fellowship registries share the Relational Disposition primary dimension with love. The secondary dimensions of love (Moral Character, Emotion-Positive, Volition) are individually shared with multiple registries across the programme. Gap: a systematic dimensional-sharing matrix across all 181 registries has not been computed; the data available here shows co-occurrence but not systematic dimension-by-dimension comparison.

**Prompt 2: What does the pattern of dimensional sharing reveal about the relationship between this characteristic and those it shares dimensions with?**

The dimensional sharing pattern that is available suggests love is the programme's most dimensionally porous characteristic — sharing dimensions with registries across all clusters, not merely C17. The Relational Disposition dimension is shared with all C17 registries (the cluster's defining dimension). The Moral Character dimension is shared with character-registries across C13-C17. The Emotion-Positive dimension is shared with joy, delight, and related affective registries. The Volition dimension is shared with will, faith, and obedience registries. This breadth suggests love is not confined to a single dimensional register but operates across the programme's full vocabulary. Gap: systematic Session D analysis needed to determine whether this breadth is characteristic of love specifically or reflects the programme's general dimensional overlap.

**Prompt 3: If dimensional sharing data is not yet available, note this explicitly.**

Systematic dimensional sharing data across all 181 registries is not available in the current data package. What is available: co-occurrence data (§5), shared anchor data (§6), and the C20 observation in SD-026. The full dimensional sharing matrix is a Session D computation task. Noted explicitly.

## Synthesis — what the cross-registry analysis reveals


### Intra-tier syntheses

**SYN-INTRA-103-006 | T6** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

Love's structural position in the programme is that of the central node in the inner-being vocabulary's relational architecture. Co-occurrence data: 196 with compassion, 105 with strength, 68 with peace, 62 with faith — confirming love's connections to the full range of C17 characteristics and extending to C20 (strength/authority). Shared anchor data: 48 verses shared across multiple registries, with Exo 34:6 (5 registries) and Gal 5:22 (5 registries) as the highest-density shared anchors — confirming love as the vocabulary cluster around which the programme's most significant cross-registry verses gather. Sequential position: love stands downstream of divine action (received) and upstream of the ethical-moral life (generative of commandment-keeping, Spirit-fruits, community identity). Causal productivity: love generates joy, rest, grief, obedience, praise — the programme's most causally productive characteristic. Vocabulary sharing: term sharing ratio 0.701, the highest in C17 — love shares vocabulary with compassion, mercy, kindness, goodness, desire, and peace. The primary gap: distinctions from adjacent characteristics (compassion, mercy, kindness) remain partially drawn; Session D is the resolution context.


### Inter-tier syntheses

**SYN-INTER-103-005 | T1, T6** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

Love's definition as the programme's most semantically comprehensive inner-being characteristic is confirmed and deepened by the structural relationship analysis. The term sharing ratio (0.701 — highest in C17) and the co-occurrence breadth (196 with compassion through to 29 with mercy) reflect the definition's conclusion: love is not a bounded standalone characteristic but the semantic centre of a cluster. The sequential finding (love as mediating-and-generative node — Q&A-105) maps onto the definition's tripartite structure: love is downstream (condition-received from God), central (disposition that organises), and upstream (act that generates all subsequent ethical life). The definition's vocabulary arc (from divine being to love of darkness — Q&A-131) is confirmed by the structural relationship data: the vocabulary extends into every adjacent registry (through XREF sharing, co-occurrence, and shared anchors), touching every corner of the programme. The structural analysis thus confirms the definitional claim: love is not merely a characteristic among others but the organising principle of the inner-being vocabulary.

**SYN-INTER-103-010 | T2, T6** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

Love's comprehensive constitutional location (all five levels) is structurally consistent with its position as the programme's central co-occurrence node. A characteristic that operates at every constitutional level will co-occur with the broadest range of other characteristics — because any verse that names soul, heart, mind, spirit, or body is potentially within love's evidential terrain. The co-occurrence breadth (top 25 covering registries from C17 through C20 and beyond) reflects the constitutional breadth: strength and authority (C20) co-occur with love because the same verses that address God's inner character include both love and power vocabulary — the divine portrait (Exo 34:6) is constitutionally comprehensive. The vocabulary sharing ratio (0.701 — highest in C17) similarly reflects the constitutional comprehensiveness: love shares vocabulary with compassion (at the somatic/racham level), with goodness (at the heart/tov level), with desire (at the ahav/phileō root level), with peace (at the soul-rest level). Constitutional breadth and vocabulary breadth are the same phenomenon viewed from different angles.

**SYN-INTER-103-014 | T3, T6** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

The faculty engagement breadth in T3 is consistent with love's structural position as the programme's central sequential node in T6. A characteristic that engages all eleven faculties will necessarily stand at the intersection of the programme's full inner-being vocabulary — because any characteristic that engages one faculty will co-occur with love insofar as love also engages that faculty. The memory-faculty engagement explains part of the co-occurrence with despair (R044 — 55 shared verses): lament appeals to the memory of God's love (Lam 3:22) as its ground. The identity-faculty engagement explains the co-occurrence with calling (R019 — 51 shared): identity-as-beloved and identity-as-called are grammatically co-ordinate (Rom 1:7). The moral-sensitivity engagement explains co-occurrence with guilt (R073 — 32 shared): moral sensitivity calibrated by love recognises guilt as the state that precedes the forgiveness that generates love (Luk 7:47). Faculty engagement and structural relationships thus form a coherent explanatory system: the faculty profile generates the co-occurrence pattern.

**SYN-INTER-103-017 | T4, T6** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

The three relational axes in T4 generate the structural relationship pattern in T6. The GOD→HUMAN axis explains love's highest co-occurrences with the programme's theological vocabulary: calling (R019 — 51 shared, identity-as-beloved and identity-as-called), faith (R059 — 62 shared, the inner condition for receiving divine love), and compassion (R023 — 196 shared, the compassionate-responsive mode of divine love). The HUMAN→HUMAN axis explains the co-occurrence with peace (R117 — 68 shared, peacemaking as love's horizontal expression) and community-related vocabulary. The sequential structure (divine love prior → human love responsive → ethical life) explains love's mediating position (Q&A-105): it stands between the GOD→HUMAN axis and the HUMAN→HUMAN axis as the transmission point. Love received from God becomes love given to neighbour — the sequential position is the relational interface described structurally. The 48 shared anchor verses are the evidential sites where these relational intersections are most visible: Exo 34:6 (GOD→HUMAN axis at maximum density), Gal 5:22 (HUMAN→HUMAN axis generated by Spirit), Joh 13:34 (HUMAN→HUMAN axis constituted by GOD→HUMAN love as model).

**SYN-INTER-103-019 | T5, T6** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

The formation process and the structural relationships are mutually reinforcing systems. The formation pathway (divine love received → Spirit infusion → soul purification → community expression) maps onto the sequential structural position: love's formation follows the same sequence as love's structural position in the programme — first received (downstream of divine action), then formed (mediating), then expressed (generative of ethical life). The growth-capacity of love (1Th 3:12 — Q&A-092) explains the programme's most puzzling co-occurrence finding: strength and authority (C20, 105 and 69 shared verses respectively). Love growing under the Lord's enabling encounters contexts of power — covenantal love among the people of God is exercised within structures of authority, governance, and strength. The love-power co-occurrence reflects the formation context: love formed within communities is formed within structures of authority. The eschatological trajectory (toward perfect love and direct knowledge) describes the structural relationships' completion: when love reaches its eschatological form, the partial sequential relationships (love received → love given → ethical life) are resolved into the direct love-knowledge of the new covenant (Jer 31:34).

**SYN-INTER-103-021 | T6, T7** *(outcome: D; structural: N/A)*

The structural relationship data and the evidential foundation are the most mutually confirming pair in the synthesis matrix. The co-occurrence breadth (T6.1) is directly explained by the vocabulary breadth (T7.1.10 — the widest semantic arc in the programme): a vocabulary that spans from divine being to love of darkness will co-occur with every other inner-being characteristic because it occupies every register of the inner-being vocabulary. The shared anchor density (48 verses — T6.6) is explained by the primary anchor's function (Lam 3:22 — T7.2.5/T7.2.6): the programme anchors the most significant cross-registry verses at love because love is the definitional centre from which adjacent characteristics derive their meaning. The vocabulary sharing ratio (0.701 — T6.4) is explained by the root-level connections (T7.1.2): the racham root connects love, compassion, and mercy; the ahav root connects love and desire; the tov root connects love and goodness. The structural relationship data is, at its core, the observable programme-level effect of the lexical architecture that the evidential foundation analysis reveals. Structural breadth and lexical breadth are the same reality at different levels of analysis.

# Chapter 6 — Open Questions

**Registry 103 — Love** · *Session D Pointer Compendium*

All open SD pointers for this registry, ordered by priority (HIGH first). This chapter is the Session D input from this word.

---

**Total open SD pointers:** 38 (HIGH: 18, MEDIUM: 20)


## HIGH priority (18)


### DIM-103-SD004

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G5368_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 1

Joh 12:25 — whoever loves his life loses it, whoever hates his life keeps it for eternal life. Agapao/miseo applied to one's own life. Structural intersection of love (103), hatred (75), soul/life (182), surrender/will (156). Question: is this verse's paradox — loving the self leads to loss, hating it leads to eternal life — the programme's clearest statement about how love and self-orientation stand in opposition as inner-being structures?


### DIM-103-SD006

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: H2617A_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 1

Mic 6:8 — chesed/justice/humility triad. The three requirements of the covenant life placed in parallel. Chesed (love/faithfulness), mishpat (justice), halak-anavah (humble walking with God). Is this the OT equivalent of the NT faith-hope-love triad? Does the programme have registries for justice and humility? If so, the Mic 6:8 triad is a cross-registry structure of fundamental importance for the programme's Session D synthesis of the moral inner life.


### DIM-103-SD007

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: H7355_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 1

Racham root (compassion) etymologically linked to rechem (womb). The womb-compassion-love nexus: divine compassion is described through womb/nursing imagery (Isa 49:15, Jer 31:20). The RACHAM root spans love (103), compassion (23), mercy (111). Question: is there a structural hierarchy within the love cluster — love as the broadest category, compassion as love-responding-to-suffering, mercy as love-overriding-just-consequence? The womb etymology grounds all three in the same bodily-relational origin.


### DIM-103-SD010

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: H3033_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 2

Jer 12:7 — 'I have given the beloved of my soul into the hands of her enemies.' God abandons his beloved Israel. The grief of abandoned love as a divine inner-being state. Does God experience grief as an inner-being characteristic? Cross-registry: love (103), grief (71), soul (182). This verse is one of the most striking in the programme for the divine inner life — God's love expressed through grief at its own loss.


### DIM-103-SD011

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G0027_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 2

Mat 3:17/Mar 12:6 — Father declares Jesus 'my beloved Son' at baptism. The Father-Son love as the archetype of all love. Does the programme have a registry examining the divine inner life as the model for the human inner life? The love between Father and Son is the origin-point from which all other love in the NT flows (Joh 17:26 — 'the love with which you loved me may be in them'). Cross-registry: love (103), grace (68), calling/identity (19), spirit cluster.


### DIM-103-SD014

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G0026_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 3

1Cor 13:4 — shared anchor with patience (116) and pride (123). The passage defines love by its relationship to patience (love is patient) and pride (love does not boast, is not arrogant). Does love produce patience as its characteristic inner quality under pressure? Or are love and patience distinct virtues sharing a common ground (the Spirit)? 1Cor 13 is the programme's richest cross-registry verse for the C17 cluster — nearly every quality named in vv.4-7 has its own registry.


### DIM-103-SD015

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G0026_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 3

Gal 5:22 — love heads the fruit-of-Spirit list. Subsequent fruits (joy, peace, patience, kindness, goodness, faithfulness, gentleness, self-control) may all be aspects or forms of love under different relational pressures. Each fruit has its own registry. Question: is love the source from which all other Spirit-fruits flow, or is it first among equals? If love generates joy, peace, patience, kindness etc., then love is not one inner virtue alongside others but the generative ground of the Spirit-formed inner life.


### DIM-103-SD016

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G0027_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 3

Mat 3:17 — Trinity nexus: Father declares love for Son as Spirit descends. The three-way structure (Father's love, Son's belovedness, Spirit's appearance) is the ground event of the NT. Cross-registry: love (103), spirit cluster, calling/identity (19). The declaration 'this is my beloved Son' grounds calling in love — identity is constituted by being-loved. Question: does the programme's calling registry (19) treat belovedness as the prior act that constitutes calling?


### DIM-103-SD018

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: H2617A_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 3

Jer 31:34 — new covenant: universal knowledge of God, no longer needing instruction from neighbour, sin forgiven, love of neighbour fulfilled eschatologically. Cross-registry nexus: love (103), knowledge/thought (160), covenant (34), forgiveness (64), calling (19). The new covenant passage integrates multiple registries into a single eschatological vision. Question: is the programme's Session D synthesis meant to reconstruct this new covenant picture from its component registries?


### DIM-103-SD019

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: H7349_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 3

Exo 34:6 divine self-declaration formula: rachum (compassionate) + grace + patience + chesed + faithfulness — five attributes of the divine inner character. The formula repeats verbatim across 8+ OT texts (Psa 86:15, 103:8, Joel 2:13, Jonah 4:2, Neh 9:17 etc.). Each attribute may have its own registry. Question: does the programme track the five-attribute formula as a cross-registry structure? The formula is the OT's most repeated self-characterisation of God and the model for human inner formation.


### DIM-103-SD020

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G2705_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 3

Luk 7:38-47 — woman's kisses and tears as bodily expression of love; Jesus' verdict: 'she loved much because she was forgiven much.' One of the most explicit causal chains in the NT: forgiveness → love. Cross-registry: love (103), forgiveness (64), grief/tears (71). Question: is love the primary inner response to forgiveness in the biblical data? If so, forgiveness may be the trigger that produces love, and love may be the inner evidence of received forgiveness.


### DIM-103-SD025

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: H7356B_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 5

H7356B rachamim has approximately 23 contamination verses from H7358 rechem (physical womb) — Gen 20:18, 29:31, 30:22, Exo 13:2-15, Num 3:12, 8:16, 18:15 etc. The semantic boundary between rachamim (compassion) and rechem (womb) is productive (Isa 49:15 uses both deliberately), but the contamination verses represent a data quality issue. Session D must work with clean rachamim data. The boundary between anatomical womb and compassion-love is itself a significant theological question: when does the bodily become the inner-being?


### DIM-103-SD027

_priority: HIGH · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G0025_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 6

Surrender/yielding (Reg 156/180) has 33/4 co-occurrence/shared anchor records with love. The self-giving pattern of agapē (Joh 15:13 — greater love has no one than to lay down his life) and surrender (giving up the self) may be the same inner movement named from different angles: love as orientation, surrender as act. Question: is surrender the inner movement that makes love possible — does love require the prior surrender of self-assertion before it can give itself to another?

## MEDIUM priority (20)


### DIM-103-SD001

_priority: MEDIUM · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-08_

*Source:* WA-DimensionReview-Instruction-v1.4-2026-04-08

Group 1581-001 (katafileō, to kiss) was assigned Emotion — Positive for the inner states expressed through the physical act. The legacy Somatic/Embodied automated label raised a genuine question about whether Scripture encodes inner-being content in bodily expression in ways the v1.4 vocabulary does not capture. Session D should assess whether a Somatic dimension is warranted across the programme — for groups where inner-being content is carried by or expressed through physical/bodily acts (kiss, embrace, prostration, anointing, tears). This may require a v1.4 vocabulary revision.


### DIM-103-SD002

_priority: MEDIUM · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-08_

*Source:* WA-DimensionReview-Instruction-v1.4-2026-04-08

C17 shows a significant concentration of Relational Disposition groups with GOD as dominant subject — the divine inner being as relationally oriented toward persons. These span love (God's chesed and agapē), compassion (divine racham), mercy (God's eleos), grace (God's charis/chen), and peace (God's shalom-giving). Combined with the Transformation/GOD groups (atonement, forgiveness, Spirit-impartation) and Moral Character/GOD groups (forgiveness as divine attribute, compassion as God's character), C17 reveals the fullest picture yet of God's inner being. Session D should synthesise across C17 and other clusters to characterise the inner being of God as Scripture describes it — his relational dispositions, inner emotional range, and what this reveals about the divine nature in relation to the human person created in his image.


### DIM-103-SD003

Mat 5:44 — love of enemies co-occurs with prayer for persecutors and promise of sonship. Agapao + seeking + will + pray intersection. The enemy-love command may be the most cross-registry verse in the NT. Registries implicated: love (103), seeking (140), will (173), pray (212). Question: does the enemy-love command require a prior orientation of seeking God (140) and an act of the will (173) as its structural preconditions?


### DIM-103-SD005

Cross-registry: love (103), desire/pleasure (43), C15 (God-orientation cluster). Question: does the philo- compound family structure reveal that love and desire share the same inner mechanism of attachment — with the moral question being solely the object toward which the attachment is directed?


### DIM-103-SD008

Eccl 9:6 — love, hatred, and envy listed as the three constitutive inner orientations that cease at death. This triadic structure may define a fundamental taxonomy of relational inner states: love (toward/for), hatred (against), envy (desiring what another has). Does the Ecclesiastes triadic structure represent the OT's most explicit inner-being taxonomy? Cross-registry: love (103), hatred (75), envy registry.


### DIM-103-SD009

Psa 25:7 — divine goodness (tuv) and steadfast love (chesed) co-present in same verse: 'according to your steadfast love remember me, for the sake of your goodness.' TOV root spans love (103) and goodness (67) cross-cluster. Question: is tuv (goodness) the content of chesed (steadfast love) — goodness as what love gives — or are they parallel divine attributes? The cross-cluster root family connection makes this one of the most structurally significant lexical bridges in the programme.


### DIM-103-SD012

The verb 'quiet' (charash — to be still, to silence) suggests love has a stilling effect on inner agitation.


### DIM-103-SD013

### DIM-103-SD017

The depth of grief reveals the depth of the prior love — grief as love's evidence under loss. Cross-registry: love (103), grief (71). Question: is grief structurally inseparable from love — not its opposite but its expression when love's object is lost? If so, love and grief may be the same inner state viewed from presence and absence.


### DIM-103-SD021

Love takes the form of disclosure: God's inner purposes made known. Cross-registry: love (103), knowledge/thought (160), mind (112), calling (19). ### DIM-103-SD022

1Th 5:8 — breastplate of faith and love, helmet of hope. The armour metaphor maps virtues anatomically: faith-and-love at the chest (vital organs), hope at the head (mind/thought). Faith-love-hope as bodily armour, each protecting a vital centre. Cross-registry: love (103), faith (59), hope (78). Question: does the faith-love-hope triad represent the programme's three primary inner orientations toward God — trust (faith), attachment (love), expectation (hope)?


### DIM-103-SD023

Act 20:37 — 'they embraced Paul and kissed him' at farewell. Somatic expression of love at anticipated permanent separation. The farewell scene is simultaneously an expression of love (embrace, kiss) and grief (weeping). Cross-registry: love (103), grief (71), fellowship/community (62). ### DIM-103-SD024

Gen 29:31 — 'when the Lord saw that Leah was hated, he opened her womb.' Hated/unloved state triggers divine compassion expressed through fertility. The womb as the site where divine love responds to human hatred. Cross-registry: love (103), hatred (75), grief (71). The unloved woman's womb being opened by God is one of the OT's most concentrated statements about divine love compensating for human lovelessness.


### DIM-103-SD026

_priority: MEDIUM · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G0025_

*Source:* WA-SessionB-Instruction-v4.7 Pass 6

C20 cluster (strength/might/authority/dominion — Regs 187/198/197/199) shares all 8 dimensions with love and co-occurs in 69-105 verses. This may be a registry-breadth effect, but the co-occurrence is genuine and large. Question: does the biblical vocabulary connect love and power structurally? Is love the form that power takes in the kingdom of God, and power the form that love takes in creation? The 1Cor 13 sequence — love as greater than gifts of power and knowledge — suggests love and power are in deliberate tension in the NT.


### DIM-103-SD028

_priority: MEDIUM · target session: D · raised: 2026-04-12 · Strong's: G0025_

Reg 73 (guilt) has 32 co-occurrences with love — unexpected and analytically significant. Luk 7:47 — 'she loved much because she was forgiven much' — establishes guilt/forgiveness as the prior state that love responds to. Question: is guilt the inner state that love-as-forgiveness-response resolves? If guilt and love are consistently co-present at the verse level, there may be a causal chain: guilt → received forgiveness → love as response. Cross-registry: love (103), guilt (73), forgiveness (64).

### SP-103-036
